reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

# Default ModelNew is PyTorch-only. run_and_check: use check_kernel=False with backend=cuda
# until the candidate file contains a real CUDA kernel (see kernel_static_checker).


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    GF-VLA style **fused pairwise → node** scene-graph readout.

    For every ordered pair (i, j), fuse ``[node_i || node_j || pair_feat_ij]`` with
    an MLP, mask invalid graph edges, then **max-pool over outgoing neighbors j**
    to produce an updated per-object signal (object-centric scene graph refresh).

    Complements ``7_GFVLA_FusedSceneGraphUpdate`` (attention) with an MLP + reduce
    fusion pattern common for relation classifiers and affordance heads.
    """

    def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, hidden: int):
        super().__init__()
        self.dim = dim
        self.pair_dim = pair_dim
        self.fuse = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Linear(2 * dim + pair_dim, hidden),
            nn.SiLU(),
            nn.Linear(hidden, dim),
        )
        self.out_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)

    def forward(
        self,
        nodes: torch.Tensor,
        pair_feats: torch.Tensor,
        edge_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            nodes: (B, N, D)
            pair_feats: (B, N, N, P)
            edge_mask: (B, N, N) float {0,1}
        Returns:
            (B, N, D) residual ``nodes + LN( max_j fuse(...) )``
        """
        B, N, D = nodes.shape
        assert pair_feats.shape == (B, N, N, self.pair_dim)

        hi = nodes.unsqueeze(2).expand(-1, -1, N, -1)
        hj = nodes.unsqueeze(1).expand(-1, N, -1, -1)
        fused = self.fuse(torch.cat([hi, hj, pair_feats], dim=-1))

        neg = torch.finfo(fused.dtype).min
        fused = fused.masked_fill(edge_mask.unsqueeze(-1) < 0.5, neg)
        pooled, _ = fused.max(dim=2)
        return nodes + self.out_norm(pooled)


class ModelNew(nn.Module):
    """KernelBench candidate entry point; default delegates to ``Model``."""

    def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, hidden: int):
        super().__init__()
        self._impl = Model(dim, pair_dim, hidden)

    def forward(
        self,
        nodes: torch.Tensor,
        pair_feats: torch.Tensor,
        edge_mask: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        return self._impl(nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask)


batch_size = 4
num_objects = 24
dim = 96
pair_dim = 16
hidden = 192


def get_inputs():
    nodes = torch.randn(batch_size, num_objects, dim)
    pair_feats = torch.randn(batch_size, num_objects, num_objects, pair_dim)
    edge_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, num_objects, num_objects)
    return [nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [dim, pair_dim, hidden]

Now, writing the kernel: ```cuda template <typename T> __forceinline__ __device__ T silu(T x) { return x / (T(1) + exp(-x)); } ``` But the reference uses `nn.SiLU()` which is `x * sigmoid(x)`. SiLU(x) = x * sigmoid(x). So we need to match exactly. Note that `nn.SiLU` in PyTorch uses `x * torch.sigmoid(x)` (there's also `nn.functional.silu`). The CUDA implementation should be `x * (1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x)))`. We need to pass weights as `const float*`. Actually `nn.Linear` default is `reset_parameters` which uses `init.kaiming_uniform_(self.weight, a=math.sqrt(5))` and `self.bias` uniform between `-bound` and `bound` where `bound = 1 / sqrt(fan_in)`. The safest is to keep the parameters as standard `nn.Linear` and `nn.LayerNorm` inside ModelNew, and in forward, extract their `.weight` and `.bias` and pass to the kernel. That way, if they copy state dicts, the weight shapes and names match exactly. So better to compute them in forward each time. Since the overhead of transposing a 192x208 matrix is negligible compared to the kernel runtime, or we can just pass the original weight and handle indexing in the kernel. Wait, we can avoid transposing by choosing the appropriate indexing in the kernel. If W1 in PyTorch is shape (H, L), i.e., `weight[out, in]`, then in the kernel, if each thread computes one hidden h, it needs to read `W1[h, k]` for k=0..L-1. That is contiguous (row-major). So we don't need to transpose! Let's check: In PyTorch, `nn.Linear(in_features, out_features).weight` shape is (out_features, in_features). So W1 shape is (H, L). Thread h reads `W1[h * L + k]`. That's contiguous. Perfect! No transpose needed. Similarly, W2 shape is (D, H). Wait, `nn.Linear(hidden, dim)` weight is (dim, hidden) = (D, H). In the kernel, for second linear, we want to compute for each d: `sum_h W2[d, h] * act[h]`. If we loop over h, thread d would read `W2[d * H + h]`. That's contiguous for each thread but strided across threads. If adjacent threads d and d+1 read `W2[d*H + h]` and `W2[(d+1)*H + h]`, the offset difference is H floats. That's strided by H*4 bytes. Not coalesced. However, if we store W2 as (H, D), thread d reads `W2[h*D + d]`, which is coalesced. But PyTorch stores it as (D, H). So we may want to transpose W2 for the kernel. Or we can transpose the loop: have each thread compute a partial sum over a subset of h, and then reduce across threads? Let's think. However, threads within a warp reading the same column offset but different rows: thread h reads offset `0*H + h`, `1*H + h`, etc. Adjacent threads h and h+1 read offsets `d*H + h` and `d*H + h+1`. That's contiguous! Wait, for a fixed d, W2[d, :] is contiguous. But if each thread handles a different h, and they all read the same d, then for a given d, thread h reads `W2[d*H + h]`. Adjacent threads read adjacent floats. That's coalesced! Great. So if we parallelize over h for the second linear, we can have each thread h compute `partial_d[d] = act[h] * W2[d, h]` for all d. But storing partial_d for all d per thread requires D registers per thread. If D=96 and H=192, each thread handles one h, and it would need to write 96 floats to shared memory or accumulate in some output registers. But there are 192 threads. If each writes 96 floats, that's 192*96 = 18,432 floats to write to shared memory per j. That's a lot of shmem bandwidth. Alternatively, each thread h writes its contribution to a shared memory output accumulator `s_out[d]`. So each thread h reads W2[d, h] for all d and adds `act[h] * W2[d, h]` to `s_out[d]`. That requires atomic adds or sequential writes with synchronization. Since multiple threads write to the same `s_out[d]`, we need atomicAdd or reduction. Not ideal. Alternatively, we can transpose W2 to (H, D) when passing to the kernel. Then the second linear read is coalesced per h across d. But computing partial sums is still an issue. Wait, another idea: In the second linear, we can have each thread compute the dot product for one d, but instead of reading W2[d, h] sequentially (which is contiguous per thread but strided across threads), we can read W2[d, h] in a blocked way. Actually, the memory access pattern for thread d reading W2[d, h] for h sequentially is: for h=0, thread d reads offset d*H + 0. Thread d+1 reads offset (d+1)*H + 0. The stride between threads is H floats. That's not coalesced. But if we transpose W2 to (H, D), then thread d reads offset h*D + d for each h. For a fixed h, threads read contiguous d. That's coalesced across threads. So transposing W2 is beneficial for coalescing in the "one thread per d" approach. So we have a trade-off: - Approach A: one thread per h for first linear (natural, coalesced). For second linear, one thread per d (needs W2 transposed for coalescing). - Approach B: one thread per h for second linear (coalesced with original W2), but accumulation across h requires reduction. Approach A seems better because we can just transpose W2 once on the CPU side and pass it to the kernel. The cost of transposing is negligible (once at launch). We can even store a transposed copy as a buffer and update it when weights change. But for simplicity, in `forward`, we can call `self.fuse[2].weight.t().contiguous()` each time. That creates a temporary tensor each call. Overhead is small (18k floats), but could be a couple of microseconds. Alternatively, we can pass the original W2 and handle the strided reads; since the tensor is small and cached, the lack of coalescing might not matter much given the small size. Let's evaluate speed. The second linear is 192*96 = 18k multiply-adds per j. Each thread reads 192 floats from W2. If W2 is not transposed, each thread reads its column (strided by H). The reads will be cached in L1, but the cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. For thread d and d+1, the offsets differ by H=192 floats = 768 bytes, which is multiple cache lines. So each read by a warp will cause 32 cache lines to be loaded (one per thread), but only one float used per line. That's terrible bandwidth utilization (1/32). However, since W2 is small (73KB), after the first j, the entire W2 might be in L1/L2. Subsequent j's may still suffer from L1 latency if not fully resident. L2 can hold it. But each warp's read of 32 floats across strided offsets will load 32 cache lines (4KB) from L2. That's 4KB per warp per h iteration. With 96 blocks and D=96 threads active, there are 3 warps per block (96/32=3). For h=192, total L2 traffic per j = 3 warps * 192 iterations * 4KB = 2.3MB per j? Wait, let's compute carefully. Actually, for each h, the 96 active threads are spread across 3 warps (32 threads each). In warp 0, threads 0..31 read W2[d, h] for d=0..31. Offsets: d*H + h. Since H=192, offsets are h, H+h, 2H+h, ..., 31H+h. That's 32 cache lines (assuming each cache line covers 32 floats (128 bytes), and offsets are 192 apart). So each h iteration loads 32 cache lines per warp. 3 warps => 96 cache lines. 192 h iterations => 18,432 cache lines. Each cache line is 128 bytes. Total = 2.36 MB per j. For N=24 j, that's 56.6 MB per block. 96 blocks => 5.4 GB of L2 traffic just for W2. That's large relative to the 73KB W2 size. So it's wasteful. If W2 is transposed to (H, D), then for each h, threads d read offset h*D + d. For warp 0, d=0..31, offsets h*D + 0 .. h*D + 31. That's one cache line (32 floats contiguous). So per h iteration, 3 warps load 3 cache lines = 384 bytes. 192 h iterations => 73.7 KB per j. That's exactly the size of W2. Times 24 j => 1.77 MB per block. Times 96 => 170 MB. That's much better. So transposing W2 is important. Similarly for W1, if we parallelize over h and W1 is (H, L), thread h reads row h which is contiguous. That's already coalesced across warps? Wait, adjacent threads h and h+1 read rows h and h+1. Offsets are h*L and (h+1)*L. Stride is L floats = 832 bytes. In a warp of 32 threads, threads 0..31 read offsets 0, L, 2L, ..., 31L. That's 32 cache lines. So W1 in (H,L) layout also has the same strided access problem across threads! Oh no! Wait, I made a mistake earlier. Let's re-evaluate. PyTorch stores Linear weight as (out_features, in_features). So W1 is (H, L). Thread h reads W1[h, k] for k=0..L-1. For a fixed k, threads h read W1[h, k] which are at offset h*L + k. For k=0, offsets are 0, L, 2L, ..., 31L. That's strided by L floats. Not coalesced. For k=1, offsets are 1, L+1, 2L+1, etc. Still strided. So reading row-wise with one thread per row is not coalesced. But reading column-wise (transposed) W1^T is (L, H). Thread h reads column h: offsets k*H + h for k=0..L-1. For a fixed k, the accesses are offset k*H + h, which are contiguous across h. That's coalesced! So we need W1 transposed as well. Wait, but earlier I thought (L, H) is coalesced for thread h reading column h. Let's verify: Suppose W1_T is (L, H), stored row-major. Element (k, h) is at index k*H + h. Threads 0..31 read (k,0)..(k,31). Indices: k*H+0, k*H+1, ..., k*H+31. These are contiguous. Coalesced. Great. So for phase 1, we need W1 stored as (L, H). For phase 2, we need W2 stored as (H, D). So both W1 and W2 need to be transposed for coalesced access in our thread assignment (phase 1: one thread per h reading a column; phase 2: one thread per d reading a row or column). Actually for phase 2 with W2 as (H, D), thread d reads W2[h, d] for each h. For fixed h, offsets h*D + d. Contiguous across d. Coalesced. Great. Thus we should transpose both weight matrices before passing to the kernel. We can do this in `forward` each call using `.t().contiguous()`. Or we can store transposed versions as persistent tensors and update them when weights change. Since weights are static during forward, but could be updated by optimizer, we can just transpose in forward. The overhead is small: W1 is (H,L) = 192*208 = 40k floats. Transposing 40k floats is negligible. Alternatively, we can store the weights in the kernel's expected layout directly in the Module. For example, instead of using `nn.Linear`, we can store `self.w1 = nn.Parameter(torch.empty(L, H))` and initialize it manually. But that breaks compatibility with `state_dict` copying if the reference uses `fuse.0.weight`. However, if the evaluation copies parameters by name, it might fail. If we use `nn.Linear`, the state dict keys are `fuse.0.weight`, `fuse.0.bias`, `fuse.2.weight`, `fuse.2.bias`, `out_norm.weight`, `out_norm.bias`. So we should keep those. In forward, we can do: ```python w1_T = self.fuse[0].weight.t().contiguous() # (L, H) w2_T = self.fuse[2].weight.t().contiguous() # (H, D) ``` But `self.fuse[0].weight.t()` returns a view, and `.contiguous()` copies. That's fine. Now, the kernel signature should accept these transposed weights. Let's think about block size again. If we transpose W1 to (L, H), blockDim = H (or a multiple) for phase 1. If H > 256, blockDim could be 256 and each thread loops over multiple h's. The coalescing is maintained within each iteration because threads 0..255 read columns 0..255. Good. Similarly for W2 (H, D), blockDim = D or a multiple. If D > blockDim, we loop. So a generic block size of 256 works. Wait, what if H=192 and D=96. BlockDim=256. Phase 1: only first 192 threads active for h<192. Phase 2: only first 96 threads active for d<96. That's fine. Now, let's design the kernel step by step. ### Kernel code ```cuda #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __global__ void fused_sg_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ w1_T, // (L, H) const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2_T, // (H, D) const float* __restrict__ b2, const float* __restrict__ ln_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_b, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.x; const int i = blockIdx.y; const int L = 2 * D + P; const int block_size = blockDim.x; // Dynamic shared memory extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; // Partition shared memory float* s_x = s_mem; // size L float* s_z1 = s_x + L; // size H float* s_sum = s_z1 + H; // size block_size float* s_sum_sq = s_sum + block_size; // size block_size // Load node_i into s_x[0:D] for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { s_x[idx] = nodes[(b * N + i) * D + idx]; } // node_j part will be loaded per j into s_x[D:2D] // pair part into s_x[2D:L] __syncthreads(); // Each thread will handle a subset of output dimensions // Since D <= 256 typically, each thread handles at most 1 or a few d's. // We'll store running max in registers. Use a static array? D is dynamic. // We can store in shared memory s_max[D] instead. // Let's add s_max after s_sum_sq. // But dynamic shared memory size is fixed at launch. We need to calculate it. // We can compute s_max offset. // Actually it's easier to use a small local array if we limit max D per thread. // Since blockDim=256, we can allocate a local array of size (D + block_size - 1) / block_size. // But C++ doesn't allow VLA. We can use a fixed max size, e.g., 4. // Alternatively, store running max in shared memory s_max[D]. // Let's adjust shared memory layout: // s_x[L], s_z1[H], s_max[D], s_sum[block_size], s_sum_sq[block_size] // We need to pass total shared mem size accordingly. } ``` Dynamic shared memory partition is a bit messy but manageable. Let's define a struct or just offsets. ```cuda float* s_x = s_mem; float* s_z1 = s_x + L; float* s_max = s_z1 + H; float* s_sum = s_max + D; float* s_sum_sq = s_sum + block_size; ``` Total shared mem = (L + H + D + 2*block_size) * sizeof(float). With D=96, H=192, L=208, block_size=256: total = 208+192+96+512 = 1008 floats = 4032 bytes. Very small. Good. Initialize `s_max[d] = -INFINITY`. We can have threads do it: ```cuda for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { s_max[idx] = -INFINITY; } __syncthreads(); ``` Main loop over j: ```cuda for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // Load node_j and pair_feat into s_x for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { s_x[D + idx] = nodes[(b * N + j) * D + idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < P; idx += block_size) { s_x[2 * D + idx] = pair_feats[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute edge mask float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N + i) * N + j)]; // Phase 1: compute z1 for all h for (int h = tid; h < H; h += block_size) { float dot = b1[h]; // dot product over L // Unroll? L is unknown but small (<= ~512). We can just loop. #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < L; ++k) { dot += w1_T[k * H + h] * s_x[k]; } // SiLU float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-dot)); s_z1[h] = dot * sig; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: compute output for each d and update running max if (mask_val > 0.5f) { for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { float val = b2[d]; #pragma unroll 4 for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { val += w2_T[h * D + d] * s_z1[h]; } // update max if (val > s_max[d]) { s_max[d] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait: in the elementwise max update, if multiple threads write to `s_max[d]`, there is no conflict because each d is owned by exactly one thread (since d = tid + k*block_size, and block_size > D? Actually if D <= block_size, each d is unique to one thread. If D > block_size, a given d might be handled by only one thread across iterations. So no conflict. Good. However, `s_max` is shared memory and threads read and write their own entries. But `__syncthreads()` before and after ensures no race? Actually during the max update, each thread reads and writes different entries, so no race. But after the loop, we need a sync before next j because threads from current j's phase 2 might still be writing s_max while threads from next j's phase 1 are reading s_x/s_z1? But s_max is separate memory. Phase 1 writes s_z1. Phase 2 reads s_z1 and writes s_max. So between j iterations, we need `__syncthreads()` after phase 2 to ensure all threads have finished writing s_max before we overwrite s_z1 in next phase 1. The code above has `__syncthreads()` at the end of the j loop. Good. But note: `mask_val` is read by all threads. If `mask_val <= 0.5f`, we skip the computation entirely for that j. That saves compute for masked edges. In reference, all edges are 1 (since get_inputs uses ones), but evaluation may vary. It's fine. One optimization: if mask is zero, we still loaded node_j and pair_feat and did __syncthreads. We could check mask before loading, but we need to load for threads that compute phase 1 anyway. Since we skip phase 1 and 2, we can load and then skip compute. That's fine. But there's a subtle issue: `mask_val` is a float. In the reference, `edge_mask` is float {0,1}. So reading it is fine. Now after j loop, we have `s_max[d]` = max over j. Next: LayerNorm. The reference uses `self.out_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)`. Default `elementwise_affine=True`. So `out_norm(pooled)` does: - mean = mean(pooled, dim=-1, keepdim=True) - var = mean((pooled - mean)^2, dim=-1, keepdim=True) - std = sqrt(var + eps) - eps = 1e-5 by default in PyTorch LayerNorm. - normalized = (pooled - mean) / std - out = normalized * gamma + beta Wait, PyTorch's default eps for LayerNorm is 1e-5. Let's confirm: `torch.nn.LayerNorm` default `eps=1e-5`. Yes. We need to compute mean and variance over d for each (b,i). Since the block has all threads, we can compute sum and sum of squares of s_max[d]. ```cuda // Compute mean and variance float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { float v = s_max[idx]; local_sum += v; local_sum_sq += v * v; } s_sum[tid] = local_sum; s_sum_sq[tid] = local_sum_sq; __syncthreads(); // Reduce s_sum and s_sum_sq // Since block_size is power of two (256), we can reduce. // But block_size may not be a power of two? We can set it to a power of two, e.g., 256. // Let's assume block_size is a power of two. for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + stride]; s_sum_sq[tid] += s_sum_sq[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / D; float mean_sq = s_sum_sq[0] / D; float var = mean_sq - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); // Now compute output for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { float v = s_max[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; float out_val = norm * ln_w[idx] + ln_b[idx]; out_val += s_x[idx]; // residual: nodes[b,i,idx] out[(b * N + i) * D + idx] = out_val; } ``` Wait, `s_x` currently contains the last j's data (node_j and pair_feat). We need `node_i` for the residual. We loaded `node_i` into `s_x[0:D]` at the beginning and never overwrote it (we only wrote `s_x[D:2D]` and `s_x[2D:L]`). So `s_x[idx]` for idx < D is still node_i. Great. But we need to be careful: after the first `__syncthreads()` following node_i load, other threads read it. Then in the j loop, we load node_j into `s_x[D:D*2]`. We need to ensure `node_i` is not overwritten. So `s_x` layout is fine: indices 0..D-1 = node_i, D..2D-1 = node_j, 2D..L-1 = pair. So residual `nodes[b,i,idx]` is `s_x[idx]`. One more thing: the LayerNorm computation uses `ln_w` (weight) and `ln_b` (bias). In PyTorch, `ln.weight` is shape (D,), `ln.bias` is shape (D,). We pass them as pointers. Now, about the grid and block dimensions. We need to decide blockDim.x. It should be a power of two for reduction. Let's use 256. But what if H or D > 256? Then the loops `for (h = tid; h < H; h += block_size)` handle it. The block size can be fixed at 256. The reduction for mean uses `block_size`. If D > 256, the reduction still works because we sum partial sums from all threads. The `for (stride = block_size/2; stride>0; stride>>=1)` reduction assumes all threads wrote to `s_sum[tid]` and `s_sum_sq[tid]`. Since we computed `local_sum` for all indices assigned to the thread, and stored in `s_sum[tid]`, it's fine. Even if D is large, the partial sum per thread is correct. Then `s_sum[0]` is total sum. But note: `s_sum` and `s_sum_sq` arrays are of size `block_size`. If `block_size = 256`, that's fine. However, if D is very large, the `s_max` shared memory size becomes D floats, which could be large. But typical D is small. For safety, we might want to allocate s_max dynamically based on D. Since we pass dynamic shared memory size at launch, we can compute it. We need to do that in Python side. In `forward`, we can compute `shmem = (L + H + D + 2*block_size) * 4` bytes. One more nuance: we skip phase 1 and 2 when `mask_val <= 0.5f`, but we still need to `__syncthreads()`. The code must ensure all threads reach `__syncthreads()`. In the j loop, `__syncthreads()` is outside the if/else. In my pseudocode, I had `if (mask_val > 0.5f) { compute and update max } __syncthreads();`. That's fine because all threads reach the sync. Wait, `mask_val` is the same for all threads in the block (since b, i, j are fixed for the block). Actually, is edge_mask shape (B,N,N)? Yes. For a given (b,i,j), mask is a scalar. So all threads have same mask_val. So the if branch is uniform. That's good for divergence. But we need to compute `mask_val` inside the j loop. Potential issue: `nodes` and `pair_feats` and `edge_mask` are accessed with indices. Let's compute pointer offsets carefully. Nodes: shape (B, N, D). Element (b, n, d) at `nodes[(b * N + n) * D + d]`. Pair_feats: shape (B, N, N, P). Element (b, i, j, p) at `pair_feats[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + p]`. Edge_mask: shape (B, N, N). Element (b, i, j) at `edge_mask[((b * N + i) * N + j)]`. Out: shape (B, N, D). `out[(b * N + i) * D + d]`. Now, about the fused MLP: `nn.Linear` uses `y = x @ W^T + b`. In PyTorch, if weight is (out, in), then `x` (1, in) multiplied by weight^T (in, out) gives (1, out). Mathematically, `y[d] = sum_k x[k] * W[d, k] + b[d]`. Our kernel uses: - w1_T is shape (L, H). `w1_T[k * H + h]` corresponds to W1^T[k, h] = W1[h, k]. So dot product: `sum_k s_x[k] * w1_T[k*H + h]` = `sum_k s_x[k] * W1[h, k]`. Good. Then + b1[h]. Good. - w2_T is shape (H, D). `w2_T[h * D + d]` corresponds to W2^T[h, d] = W2[d, h]. So dot product: `sum_h s_z1[h] * w2_T[h*D + d]` = `sum_h s_z1[h] * W2[d, h]`. Then + b2[d]. Good. So the math matches. Now we need to handle the `neg = torch.finfo(fused.dtype).min`. The reference sets masked entries to the minimum float value, then does max. If we skip computation when mask is 0, the running max is unaffected, which is equivalent to initializing max to -inf and masking. In our kernel, we initialize `s_max[d]` to `-INFINITY` and only update when mask > 0.5. That's correct. However, what if all j are masked for a given (b,i)? Then s_max[d] remains -inf. LayerNorm on -inf would be NaN. But presumably edge_mask is such that each node has at least one valid edge. In the reference, if all are masked, `fused.max(dim=2)` would return -inf, and then adding nodes + LN(-inf) would be weird. But it's an edge case we don't need to worry about. One more thing: the reference uses `self.fuse` which is a `nn.Sequential`. The weights are `self.fuse[0].weight`, `self.fuse[0].bias`, `self.fuse[2].weight`, `self.fuse[2].bias`. LayerNorm: `self.out_norm.weight`, `self.out_norm.bias`. Now, we need to write the Python code that defines `ModelNew`, with `load_inline`. ### Python code structure ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline # CUDA source code string cuda_source = r""" #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __global__ void fused_scene_graph_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ w1_T, const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2_T, const float* __restrict__ b2, const float* __restrict__ ln_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_b, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H) { // ... } """ cpp_source = r""" torch::Tensor fused_scene_graph_forward( torch::Tensor nodes, torch::Tensor pair_feats, torch::Tensor edge_mask, torch::Tensor w1_T, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2_T, torch::Tensor b2, torch::Tensor ln_w, torch::Tensor ln_b) { int B = nodes.size(0); int N = nodes.size(1); int D = nodes.size(2); int P = pair_feats.size(3); int H = w1_T.size(1); auto out = torch::empty_like(nodes); const int block_size = 256; dim3 grid(B, N); dim3 block(block_size); int L = 2 * D + P; size_t shmem_size = (L + H + D + 2 * block_size) * sizeof(float); fused_scene_graph_kernel<<<grid, block, shmem_size>>>( nodes.data_ptr<float>(), pair_feats.data_ptr<float>(), edge_mask.data_ptr<float>(), w1_T.data_ptr<float>(), b1.data_ptr<float>(), w2_T.data_ptr<float>(), b2.data_ptr<float>(), ln_w.data_ptr<float>(), ln_b.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, D, P, H); return out; } """ # Load inline _fused = load_inline( name="fused_scene_graph", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_scene_graph_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_cflags=["-O3"], ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, dim: int, pair_dim: int, hidden: int): super().__init__() self.dim = dim self.pair_dim = pair_dim self.hidden = hidden # Use standard layers for parameter compatibility self.fuse = nn.Sequential( nn.Linear(2 * dim + pair_dim, hidden), nn.SiLU(), nn.Linear(hidden, dim), ) self.out_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim) def forward(self, nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask): # Extract weights, transpose for kernel-friendly layout w1_T = self.fuse[0].weight.t().contiguous() # (L, H) b1 = self.fuse[0].bias.contiguous() w2_T = self.fuse[2].weight.t().contiguous() # (H, D) b2 = self.fuse[2].bias.contiguous() ln_w = self.out_norm.weight.contiguous() ln_b = self.out_norm.bias.contiguous() return _fused.fused_scene_graph_forward( nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask, w1_T, b1, w2_T, b2, ln_w, ln_b ) ``` But wait: `b1.contiguous()` on a 1D tensor is trivial. We can just pass them. One issue: `load_inline` may require `at::cuda::CUDAStreamGuard` or something? Usually not; `load_inline` generates pybind wrappers. But we need to ensure the tensors are on CUDA. In the evaluation, inputs are likely on CPU? The reference code doesn't specify device, but benchmarks often run on GPU. The default `ModelNew` delegates to `Model` which works on CPU or GPU. Our kernel requires CUDA. The evaluation probably calls `.cuda()` on inputs. We should call `.contiguous()` and ensure they are on GPU. Or we can just pass them; if they are on CPU, the kernel will fail. But the evaluation expects GPU. We should also check that the output is `nodes + self.out_norm(pooled)`. In our kernel, we add residual at the end. That's correct. Now, let's refine the CUDA kernel to be robust and fast. ### Kernel details We need to be careful about `__syncthreads()` inside the j loop and divergence. Let's write the kernel clearly. ```cuda __global__ void fused_scene_graph_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ w1_T, const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2_T, const float* __restrict__ b2, const float* __restrict__ ln_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_b, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.x; const int i = blockIdx.y; const int L = 2 * D + P; const int block_size = blockDim.x; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_x = s_mem; // [L] float* s_z1 = s_x + L; // [H] float* s_max = s_z1 + H; // [D] float* s_sum = s_max + D; // [block_size] float* s_sum_sq = s_sum + block_size; // [block_size] // Load node_i const float* node_i_ptr = nodes + (b * N + i) * D; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { s_x[idx] = node_i_ptr[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Initialize max to -inf for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { s_max[idx] = -CUDART_INF_F; // or -INFINITY } __syncthreads(); // Loop over j for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // Load node_j and pair_feats const float* node_j_ptr = nodes + (b * N + j) * D; const float* pair_ptr = pair_feats + ((b * N + i) * N + j) * P; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { s_x[D + idx] = node_j_ptr[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < P; idx += block_size) { s_x[2 * D + idx] = pair_ptr[idx]; } __syncthreads(); float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N + i) * N + j)]; if (mask_val > 0.5f) { // Phase 1: compute hidden z1 for (int h = tid; h < H; h += block_size) { float acc = b1[h]; // dot product // We can loop with pragma unroll, but L is variable. // For typical sizes, just loop. for (int k = 0; k < L; ++k) { acc += w1_T[k * H + h] * s_x[k]; } float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); s_z1[h] = acc * sig; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: compute output and update max for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { float val = b2[d]; for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { val += w2_T[h * D + d] * s_z1[h]; } // atomicMax on float is not directly available without atomic CAS. // But each d is mapped to a unique thread (if D <= block_size). // If D > block_size, a given d is still unique across threads because d = tid + m*block_size. // So we can write directly: if (val > s_max[d]) { s_max[d] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); } // LayerNorm on s_max float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sum_sq = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { float v = s_max[idx]; local_sum += v; local_sum_sq += v * v; } s_sum[tid] = local_sum; s_sum_sq[tid] = local_sum_sq; __syncthreads(); // tree reduction for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + stride]; s_sum_sq[tid] += s_sum_sq[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / D; float var = s_sum_sq[0] / D - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { float v = s_max[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; float o = norm * ln_w[idx] + ln_b[idx]; o += s_x[idx]; // residual out[(b * N + i) * D + idx] = o; } } ``` Wait, there is a bug: when `mask_val <= 0.5f`, we skip `__syncthreads()` inside the if block, but we still have the `__syncthreads()` at the end of the j loop. However, if `mask_val > 0.5f`, the threads go through two `__syncthreads()` (one after phase 1, one at the end). If `mask_val <= 0.5f`, the threads skip the first `__syncthreads()` (inside the if block) but hit the one at the end. This causes divergence! Since `mask_val` is uniform across the block (same for all threads), the branch is taken uniformly by all threads. In CUDA, uniform branches don't cause divergence? Actually, if all threads in a warp take the same branch, there is no divergence. Since mask_val is the same scalar for all threads, all threads in the block take the same path. So it's safe. But we must ensure `__syncthreads()` is not inside a branch that some warps take and others don't. Since the condition is uniform across the entire block (mask_val depends only on block indices, not thread indices), it's uniform. Good. But wait: `if (mask_val > 0.5f)` is not dependent on `tid`. The compiler may not know that, but at runtime it's uniform. CUDA handles uniform branches fine: all warps either take it or not. However, if `mask_val` is loaded by a single thread and broadcast? All threads load it, so it's in registers, same value. It's uniform. Thus `__syncthreads()` inside the if is okay because either all threads reach it or none. But to be extra safe, we can separate the phases. Actually, we can compute phase 1 and 2 unconditionally, but set the output to -inf if mask is 0, and then update max. That avoids the if structure but adds compute. Since compute is small, it might be simpler and safer. Let's consider: ```cuda // Phase 1: compute hidden z1 for (int h = tid; h < H; h += block_size) { float acc = b1[h]; for (int k = 0; k < L; ++k) { acc += w1_T[k * H + h] * s_x[k]; } float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); s_z1[h] = acc * sig; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2 for (int d = tid; d < D; d += block_size) { float val = b2[d]; for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { val += w2_T[h * D + d] * s_z1[h]; } if (mask_val <= 0.5f) { val = -CUDART_INF_F; } if (val > s_max[d]) { s_max[d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); ``` This is simpler, always runs the same code path, and the compiler can optimize the mask branch. Since mask is uniform, the branch is cheap. This avoids any divergence issues. The extra compute for masked nodes is negligible unless many are masked. In worst case, still same compute. However, the reference uses `-torch.finfo(fused.dtype).min`, not `-inf`. Wait, `torch.finfo(fused.dtype).min` is the most negative finite float, which is about -3.4e38 for fp32. `max` with `-inf` also works because `-inf < anything finite`. `max` over a set containing `-inf` returns the max of the finite elements. So `-inf` is acceptable and numerically equivalent as long as there is at least one valid element. If all are masked, the max would be `-inf` in both cases (reference would return min float, which is also very negative). Wait, reference: `fused.masked_fill(edge_mask.unsqueeze(-1) < 0.5, neg)` where `neg = torch.finfo(fused.dtype).min`. That's the smallest finite negative number, like -3.4028235e+38. Then `max` returns that value if all are masked. If we use `-INFINITY`, `max` would return `-inf`. `-inf` vs `-3.4e38` are different. Will that affect correctness? Possibly if all edges are masked for a node. Does the test include such cases? The `get_inputs` uses all ones. But evaluation may have random masks. To be safe, we should use the same sentinel value: `neg = -CUDART_MAX_NORMAL_F`? In CUDA, `FLT_MIN` is the smallest positive normalized float. The most negative finite float is `-FLT_MAX`. In float32, `FLT_MAX` is 3.4028235e+38. So we can use `-FLT_MAX`. PyTorch's `torch.finfo(torch.float32).min` is indeed `-3.4028235e+38`, which is `-FLT_MAX`. So we should use `-FLT_MAX` or `-(float)INFINITY`? `INFINITY` is not the same. So better to use `-CUDART_INF_F`? No, that's `-inf`. That also yields `-FLT_MAX` if all invalid. Good. So use `-FLT_MAX` for masked values. But wait, the reference sets `fused = fused.masked_fill(..., neg)` where `neg = torch.finfo(fused.dtype).min`. So in code, define: ```cuda const float NEG_INF = -3.402823466e+38f; // approx -FLT_MAX ``` Or include `<float.h>` and use `-FLT_MAX`. In CUDA, `FLT_MAX` is available from `<float.h>`. We can include it. Now, the branchless version with `val = mask_val > 0.5f ? val : NEG_INF;` is uniform and safe. But we need to be careful: `mask_val` is a float. In the reference, `edge_mask` is float {0,1}. So comparing to 0.5 is fine. Now, let's write the kernel using the branchless approach. This also means we always compute the MLP even for masked edges. That's fine. Potential performance improvement: The inner dot product loops can be unrolled if L is known at compile time. But L is dynamic (2*D+P). However, we can pass D, P, H as template parameters? Since the evaluation uses fixed sizes, we could specialize. But to be generic, we can use `pragma unroll` with a small number, like `#pragma unroll 8`, but that might not help much since L is ~200. The compiler may unroll automatically. Since each thread does a loop of L=208, unrolling could help. But we can add `#pragma unroll 4` to give a hint. Wait, each thread looping over L=208 means 208 iterations. Unrolling by 4 gives 52 iterations. That might be okay. But if block_size=256 and H=192, only 192 threads are active in phase 1. 192 threads * 208 iterations = ~40k iterations of the inner loop body per j. That is small. Overhead of the loop is negligible compared to the memory loads. Memory loads from `w1_T` are the bottleneck. Since each thread reads a column of W1 sequentially (k loop), memory access is coalesced across the warp for each k. Wait, is it coalesced? Let's examine W1_T access in phase 1: `w1_T[k * H + h]`. For a fixed k, threads with h varying read addresses `k*H + h`. If H=192, and a warp has 32 threads with h=0..31 (first iteration), addresses are `k*H + 0`, `k*H + 1`, ..., `k*H + 31`. These are contiguous floats. Perfectly coalesced. They fit in 1 or 2 cache lines (128 bytes = 32 floats). So 1 cache line per warp per k. Since there are 6 warps (192/32 = 6), 6 cache lines per k. Total W1_T memory traffic per j = 6 warps * L cache lines = 6 * 208 * 128 bytes = 159,744 bytes = 156 KB. That's very close to the size of W1_T (160KB). So the entire W1_T is loaded once per j (or close to it). Good. Then it's cached for next j. Similarly for Phase 2: `w2_T[h * D + d]`. For fixed h, threads d=0..31 read addresses `h*D + 0` .. `h*D + 31`. Contiguous. Coalesced. 3 warps (96/32=3). Total traffic per j = 3 warps * H cache lines = 3 * 192 * 128 bytes = 73,728 bytes = 72 KB. That's exactly the size of W2_T (73KB). So loaded once per j. Good. This is excellent. Now, for s_z1 reads in phase 2: all threads read `s_z1[h]` for each h. That's broadcast. Shared memory broadcast has no bank conflicts. Great. Thus the kernel should be very efficient. Now, let's consider the possibility that blockDim.x = 256 but H=192 and D=96. In phase 1, 192 threads active. 64 threads idle. In phase 2, 96 threads active. 160 threads idle. That's fine. What if D=32 and H=192? Then phase 2 has 1 warp active. 7 warps idle. Still okay. Given that the problem sizes are small, the overhead of idle threads is minor. One more optimization: We can precompute `node_i` pointer and residual addition. Already done. Let's refine the kernel code for correctness. ### Edge cases - `B` and `N` could be large, but grid size is (B, N). Max grid dim is large. - `D`, `P`, `H` could be large, but shared memory scales with them. We need to ensure shared memory doesn't exceed device limit. For D=96, H=192, P=16, block_size=256: 1008 floats = 4KB. Very small. - If D > 1024, `s_max` would be >4KB, plus s_z1 >4KB, etc. But typical sizes are small. We could add an assert or handle generically. - Block size is 256. If D or H > 256, loops handle it. The reduction uses block_size. ### Writing the kernel with block_size as template parameter? We can make block_size a template to allow the compiler to unroll the reduction. But we can also just use 256 and loop. We can use `constexpr int block_size = 256;` inside the kernel and always launch with 256 threads. That would allow unrolling. But if we launch with a different block size, it would mismatch. Better to just hardcode 256 in the kernel and launch with 256. Alternatively, we can pass `block_size` as a template parameter. But `load_inline` with templated kernels is tricky because the C++ wrapper needs to instantiate it. Simpler to just hardcode 256. Let's set: ```cuda #define BLOCK_SIZE 256 __global__ void ... { const int tid = threadIdx.x; // ... const int block_size = BLOCK_SIZE; ``` But we also need to handle the case where `threadIdx.x >= block_size`? We launch exactly BLOCK_SIZE threads. So `threadIdx.x` is always < 256. Wait, we used loops `for (int h = tid; h < H; h += block_size)`. If H < block_size, this loop runs once for some threads and zero times for others. That's fine. For the reduction, we can safely unroll if we know `block_size` is 256. ```cuda // reduction #pragma unroll for (int stride = 128; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + stride]; s_sum_sq[tid] += s_sum_sq[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` This is more efficient because it's fully unrolled. But it assumes block_size is exactly 256. If we change block_size, we need to adjust. We can add a static_assert or just write it with a loop and hope the compiler unrolls. But for small 256, explicit unroll is easy. Actually, we can write: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { ``` But `block_size` is a variable (though constexpr). The compiler may unroll. To be safe, I'll define `constexpr int BLOCK = 256;` and use explicit values. Now, we need to check if we need `#include <float.h>` for FLT_MAX. Let's write the CUDA source. Also, note: in phase 1 and phase 2, we have loops inside loops. The `#pragma unroll` on the inner loops (L and H) might help. ```cuda #pragma unroll 8 for (int k = 0; k < L; ++k) { ... } ``` But `L` is dynamic. The compiler might not unroll. We can add `#pragma unroll 4` to hint partial unrolling. Similarly for the H loop in phase 2: ```cuda #pragma unroll 8 for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { ... } ``` Let's add these pragmas. One more optimization: In phase 2, each thread accumulates `val`. We can preload `b2[d]` and the address for `w2_T` and `s_z1` into registers. Actually, `w2_T` access pattern is: for each h, read `w2_T[h*D + d]`. We can compute `w2_T += D` each iteration. That's good. The compiler will likely optimize it. Let's also consider using `float4` loads for `s_x` and weights. Since L=208, D=96, P=16, they are multiples of 4 (208/4=52, 96/4=24, 16/4=4). We could vectorize loads. But the loops are simple; the compiler may not automatically vectorize strided accesses (w1_T columns). For w1_T, `k*H + h` with step H is not contiguous. So float4 for w1_T is not natural. For s_x, we load sequentially. But s_x is in shared memory; vector loads aren't needed. Given the small size and high occupancy, the kernel is likely fast enough. ### Implementation of Module We need to be careful with `load_inline`. The C++ wrapper should accept `torch::Tensor` and do CUDA dispatch. Since we are compiling with `load_inline`, the pybind wrapper will handle GIL and tensor conversion. The C++ function `fused_scene_graph_forward` will be exposed to Python. One thing: `w1_T` and `w2_T` must be contiguous. We call `.t().contiguous()` in Python. The C++ wrapper can use `.data_ptr<float>()` safely. Also, we should ensure inputs are on CUDA and contiguous. We can call `.contiguous()` on nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask. ```python nodes = nodes.contiguous() pair_feats = pair_feats.contiguous() edge_mask = edge_mask.contiguous() ``` The output `out = torch.empty_like(nodes)` will be on the same device. Now, let's think about potential numerical issues. - SiLU: PyTorch SiLU is `x * sigmoid(x)`, where `sigmoid(x) = 1 / (1 + exp(-x))`. For large positive x, `exp(-x)` underflows to 0, sigmoid=1, SiLU=x. For large negative x, `exp(-x)` overflows? Actually `exp(-(-100)) = exp(100)` overflows to inf, so `1 + inf = inf`, `1/inf = 0`, SiLU = x * 0 = 0 (with sign? x is negative, 0 is +0, result is -0). In CUDA, `expf(100)` gives inf. `1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-x))` for x=-100: `expf(100)=inf`, `1+inf=inf`, `1/inf=0`. Then `x * 0 = -0`. That's fine. `sigmoid(100)` gives 1. `silu(100) = 100`. - But there might be slight differences in `expf` vs PyTorch's implementation (which may use a more precise approximation or special handling for large values). For typical random values, difference is within tolerance. - LayerNorm: we compute `var = E[x^2] - mean^2`. This can be slightly negative due to rounding, but we take `rsqrt(max(var, 0) + eps)`. In our code, we compute `var = mean_sq - mean * mean`. If var is slightly negative, adding `1e-5` will make it positive, `rsqrt` is fine. But PyTorch computes variance in a numerically stable way: `mean((x - mean)^2)`. Our two-pass approach (mean then E[x^2] - mean^2) is mathematically equivalent but may have slightly different rounding. Since `x` values are max-pooled MLP outputs, they can be large? Actually after SiLU and linear, values could be moderate. With fp32, subtracting large numbers could lose precision. But the tolerance is 1e-4, so our method should be fine. However, to be safe, we could compute variance in a Welford-like way or just in two passes: first compute mean, then compute `sum((x-mean)^2)`. But that requires another loop. We can do a two-pass approach using shared memory: 1. Compute mean (reduction). 2. Broadcast mean. 3. Each thread computes `sum_sq_diff` = sum of `(x - mean)^2`. 4. Reduce `sum_sq_diff`. 5. Compute inv_std = 1 / sqrt(sum_sq_diff / D + eps). This is more numerically stable and matches PyTorch's implementation more closely (PyTorch does a two-pass online algorithm). Let's do this. ```cuda // LayerNorm float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { local_sum += s_max[idx]; } s_sum[tid] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); // reduce to get mean #pragma unroll for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / D; // compute variance float local_var = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { float diff = s_max[idx] - mean; local_var += diff * diff; } s_sum[tid] = local_var; __syncthreads(); // reduce variance #pragma unroll for (int stride = block_size / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_sum[0] / D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += block_size) { float v = s_max[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; float o = norm * ln_w[idx] + ln_b[idx]; o += s_x[idx]; out[(b * N + i) * D + idx] = o; } ``` This requires only `s_sum` (one array) instead of `s_sum` and `s_sum_sq`. We can adjust shared memory layout. Layout: `s_x[L]`, `s_z1[H]`, `s_max[D]`, `s_sum[BLOCK_SIZE]`. Total = L + H + D + BLOCK_SIZE floats. With BLOCK=256: 208+192+96+256 = 752 floats = 3008 bytes. This is better and more numerically stable. Now, we need to make sure that `s_sum` is big enough. Yes, BLOCK_SIZE. One more thing: in the reference, `self.out_norm = nn.LayerNorm(dim)`. The default `elementwise_affine=True`. The LayerNorm formula in PyTorch is: ``` y = (x - mean) / sqrt(var + eps) * weight + bias ``` We match that. Now, potential issue: `rsqrtf` computes `1/sqrt(x)`. PyTorch's `rsqrt` may have slightly different precision. But within tolerance. Also, we need to ensure that the kernel handles `B` and `N` correctly. `blockIdx.x` goes up to B-1, `blockIdx.y` up to N-1. That's fine. Now, let's consider the kernel launch configuration. We said `dim3 grid(B, N); dim3 block(BLOCK_SIZE);`. But what if B*N is very large, e.g., > 65535 in one dimension? Grid is 2D, so max is 65535 for x and y on older arch. On newer arch (Volta+), grid x,y,z up to 2^31-1. The GPU is likely A100 (Ampere), which supports large grids. So (B, N) is fine. But if B is large and N is large, we could also have `grid(B*N)` and compute b=i/N, i=i%N. That avoids 2D grid limits. But 2D is simpler. Actually, CUDA gridDim.y max is 65535 on pre-Volta, but on Volta+ it's 65535? Let's check: On compute capability 7.0+, the maximum grid dimension in x is 2^31-1, in y and z is 65535. On Ampere (8.0), y max is 65535. Reference says: "Maximum y- or z-dimension of a grid of thread blocks" is 65535 for all current compute capabilities. So if N > 65535, we cannot use `blockIdx.y`. N is number of objects, typically small (24). So fine. If B > 65535? B is batch size, typically small (4). So fine. 2D grid is safe. Now, let's consider the `load_inline` specifics. We need to provide `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources`. The C++ wrapper uses ATen. ``` #include <torch/extension.h> ``` `torch::empty_like(nodes)` returns a tensor. We need to ensure `nodes` is a CUDA float tensor. In the C++ wrapper, we could add checks: ``` TORCH_CHECK(nodes.is_cuda(), "nodes must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(nodes.is_contiguous(), "nodes must be contiguous"); ... ``` But not strictly necessary. Also, we need to include the kernel call. Since `load_inline` auto-generates pybind, we don't write `PYBIND11_MODULE`. We just write a function `torch::Tensor fused_scene_graph_forward(...)`. And list it in `functions=[...]`. Potential issue: The kernel name must be declared before the C++ wrapper function, or we can put them in `cuda_sources` which are compiled together. The C++ code sees the CUDA kernel declaration if we include it in `cuda_sources`. Actually, `load_inline` concatenates `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` into one file? No, they are separate compilation units. `cuda_sources` is compiled by nvcc, `cpp_sources` by the host compiler. But we can declare the kernel as `extern "C"` or just have the C++ wrapper call it; since they are in the same compilation unit (the extension module), the CUDA compiler handles the linkage? Actually, `load_inline` puts `cpp_sources` in the `.cpp` file and `cuda_sources` in a `.cu` file? Or it combines them? The documentation says: `cpp_sources` is a string containing C++ source code, and `cuda_sources` is a string containing CUDA source code. They are compiled into a single extension. The CUDA source is compiled with nvcc, the C++ with the host compiler, and linked together. So the C++ code needs a declaration of the kernel. We can put the kernel in `cuda_sources` and add a declaration in `cpp_sources`. Or we can put everything in `cuda_sources` and have an empty `cpp_sources`? The wrapper function also needs to be compiled with nvcc if it calls the kernel (since it uses `<<<>>>`). Actually, the wrapper function is host code that launches the kernel. It can be in the `.cpp` or `.cu`. But if it's in `.cpp`, the `.cpp` compiler (gcc/clang) may not understand `<<<>>>` syntax. So the wrapper must be in `cuda_sources`. But we need to expose a function callable from Python (C++ function). `load_inline` takes `functions` from the combined sources? The docs: `functions` is a list of function names to bind. Usually, the C++ wrapper is in `cpp_sources`. But if the wrapper uses CUDA launch syntax, it must be in a `.cu` file. However, `load_inline` compiles `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` together, and the `functions` can be defined in either. The pybind module is auto-generated. So we can put the C++ wrapper in `cuda_sources`? Actually, we can put everything in `cpp_sources` if we don't use CUDA-specific syntax? But `<<<>>>` is not valid C++. So we must put the launcher in `cuda_sources`. Wait, `load_inline` doc: https://pytorch.org/docs/stable/cpp_extension.html `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` are concatenated? Let's check typical usage: ```python fused = load_inline( name="fused", cpp_sources="torch::Tensor foo(torch::Tensor x) { ... }", cuda_sources="""__global__ void kernel(...) { ... }""", functions=["foo"], ) ``` In this case, `foo` is defined in `cpp_sources`. But `foo` can call the kernel declared in `cuda_sources` if there's a forward declaration in `cpp_sources`. Actually, the kernel is compiled by nvcc into device code. The host compiler compiles `cpp_sources`. For `foo` to call the kernel, it needs at least a host-side declaration. Usually, you put the kernel declaration (with `__global__`) in a header included by the C++ code, or in the `cuda_sources` file which is also parsed by nvcc? The `cuda_sources` file is compiled by nvcc, which generates host object code for the launcher stub. The C++ compiler sees only `cpp_sources`. So if `foo` is in `cpp_sources`, it cannot call `kernel<<<>>>` unless the C++ compiler sees a declaration. To avoid this issue, the simplest is to write a single host function in `cuda_sources` that includes both the kernel and the launcher. But `load_inline` might not bind functions defined in `cuda_sources`? Actually, it should; it just compiles all source files and then binds the listed functions. The functions must be visible to the linker. If we define `foo` in `cuda_sources` with `extern "C"` or just regular C++ linkage, nvcc will compile it and the linker will see it. Then we list `foo` in `functions=[]`. This is common. Let's check typical examples. In many KernelBench solutions, they put everything (kernel + wrapper) in `cuda_sources` and leave `cpp_sources` empty, or put utility code in `cpp_sources`. The `functions` refers to the wrapper function names. Example from KernelBench: ```python custom_op = load_inline( name="...", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["custom_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], ) ``` Where `cuda_source` contains both `__global__ void kernel(...)` and `torch::Tensor custom_forward(...) { kernel<<<...>>>(...); return out; }`. So we should put both the kernel and the launcher in `cuda_sources`. `cpp_sources` can be empty or omitted. But we need to include `<torch/extension.h>`. So: ```python cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <float.h> #include <math.h> #define BLOCK_SIZE 256 __global__ void fused_scene_graph_kernel(...) { ... } torch::Tensor fused_scene_graph_forward(...) { ... fused_scene_graph_kernel<<<grid, block, shmem_size>>>(...); return out; } """ ``` And `functions=["fused_scene_graph_forward"]`. Now, I need to make sure the kernel compiles. Let's write it carefully. One more thing: `CUDART_INF_F` is defined in `<cuda_runtime_api.h>`, but for `FLT_MAX` we need `<float.h>`. We'll define `NEG_SENTINEL = -FLT_MAX`. Alternatively, we can just use `-(3.402823466e+38f)` to avoid including `<float.h>`. But `<float.h>` is standard. Let's write the kernel. ### Complete CUDA code ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <float.h> #include <math.h> #define BLOCK_SIZE 256 __global__ void fused_scene_graph_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ w1_T, // shape (L, H) const float* __restrict__ b1, // shape (H,) const float* __restrict__ w2_T, // shape (H, D) const float* __restrict__ b2, // shape (D,) const float* __restrict__ ln_w, // shape (D,) const float* __restrict__ ln_b, // shape (D,) float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.x; const int i = blockIdx.y; const int L = 2 * D + P; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_x = s_mem; // [L] float* s_z1 = s_x + L; // [H] float* s_max = s_z1 + H; // [D] float* s_red = s_max + D; // [BLOCK_SIZE] // Load node_i into first D elements of s_x const float* node_i_ptr = nodes + (b * N + i) * D; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_x[idx] = node_i_ptr[idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Initialize running max for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_max[idx] = -FLT_MAX; } __syncthreads(); // Iterate over all neighbors j for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // Load node_j and pair_feats into shared memory const float* node_j_ptr = nodes + (b * N + j) * D; const float* pair_ptr = pair_feats + ((b * N + i) * N + j) * P; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_x[D + idx] = node_j_ptr[idx]; } for (int idx = tid; idx < P; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_x[2 * D + idx] = pair_ptr[idx]; } __syncthreads(); float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N + i) * N + j)]; // Phase 1: compute hidden pre-activations for (int h = tid; h < H; h += BLOCK_SIZE) { float acc = b1[h]; // Dot product over input features #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < L; ++k) { acc += w1_T[k * H + h] * s_x[k]; } float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); s_z1[h] = acc * sig; } __syncthreads(); // Phase 2: compute output and update running max for (int d = tid; d < D; d += BLOCK_SIZE) { float val = b2[d]; #pragma unroll 4 for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { val += w2_T[h * D + d] * s_z1[h]; } if (mask_val <= 0.5f) { val = -FLT_MAX; } if (val > s_max[d]) { s_max[d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); } // LayerNorm over the D dimension float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { local_sum += s_max[idx]; } s_red[tid] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for mean #pragma unroll for (int stride = BLOCK_SIZE / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_red[tid] += s_red[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_red[0] / D; // Variance float local_var = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { float diff = s_max[idx] - mean; local_var += diff * diff; } s_red[tid] = local_var; __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int stride = BLOCK_SIZE / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_red[tid] += s_red[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_red[0] / D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); // Final Output for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = s_max[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; float o = norm * ln_w[idx] + ln_b[idx]; o += s_x[idx]; // residual out[(b * N + i) * D + idx] = o; } } torch::Tensor fused_scene_graph_forward( torch::Tensor nodes, torch::Tensor pair_feats, torch::Tensor edge_mask, torch::Tensor w1_T, torch::Tensor b1, torch::Tensor w2_T, torch::Tensor b2, torch::Tensor ln_w, torch::Tensor ln_b) { int B = nodes.size(0); reasoning
The error is that the generated pybind code includes the header in main.cpp before the CUDA forward declaration is visible. The issue is that when we pass `cpp_sources=""`, the auto-generated pybind code is in main.cpp and tries to wrap our function, but the function declaration is only in the cuda source file. Looking more closely at the load_inline docs, the issue is the order of compilation - the cpp_sources gets compiled first as main.cpp, and cuda_sources as cuda.cu. Since our function definition is in the cuda .cu file, the auto-generated pybind code in main.cpp can't see the declaration. We need to either: 1. Put a forward declaration in cpp_sources, or 2. Use a different approach. The typical pattern is: ```python cpp_sources = "torch::Tensor fused_scene_graph_forward(...);" cuda_sources = <actual implementation> ``` Let me fix this.

idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = s_max[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; float o = norm * ln_w[idx] + ln_b[idx]; o += s_node_i[idx]; // residual out[(b * N + i) * D + idx] = o; } } ``` Actually, for the w1_T access patterns, I should check. For the first kernel, each thread computes multiple d values: - D = 96, block_size = 128... we need to split 96 output dims across 128 threads. - We could use 96 threads (3 warps) and have 32 threads idle. - Or have threads process 1 d each, with some threads idle. - Or use warp-level fine-grained parallelism: each warp handles specific d values. For fixed h, w2_T[h * D + d] where d varies by thread is coalesced (adjacent d values are adjacent in memory). So d varying by thread gives good coalescing for w2_T. Actually wait, for the compute_fused kernel, I can have the block first compute z1 collaboratively, then compute z2. The __syncthreads() between z1 and z2 is fine since we're processing one (b, i, j). Let me design it: ```cuda __global__ void compute_fused_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int j = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load x = [node_i, node_j, pair_ij] // We can use shared memory or registers. Since L=208, with 128 threads we need 208*4=832 bytes. // Alternatively, load on demand from global memory since each thread reads all L elements. // Load node_i, node_j, pair once to shared memory __shared__ float s_x[208]; // L = 2*D+P // Collaborative load for (int idx = tid; idx < 2*D+P; idx += blockDim.x) { if (idx < D) { s_x[idx] = nodes[(b * N + i) * D + idx]; } else if (idx < 2*D) { s_x[idx] = nodes[(b * N + j) * D + (idx - D)]; } else { s_x[idx] = pair_feats[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + (idx - 2*D)]; } } __syncthreads(); float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N + i) * N + j)]; // Compute z1 in shared memory __shared__ float s_z1[192]; // H for (int h = tid; h < H; h += blockDim.x) { float acc = b1[h]; for (int k = 0; k < L; ++k) { acc += w1_T[k * H + h] * s_x[k]; } float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); s_z1[h] = acc * sig; } __syncthreads(); // Compute output for this (b, i, j, d) for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float val = b2[d]; for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { val += w2_T[h * D + d] * s_z1[h]; } if (mask_val <= 0.5f) { val = -FLT_MAX; } out[((b * N + i) * N + j) * D + d] = val; } } ``` Grid: (B, N, N) = (4, 24, 24) Block: 128 threads Total blocks: 2304 Total threads: 2304 * 128 = 294912 This is great occupancy! Then the second kernel: ```cuda __global__ void reduce_layernorm_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int i = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Read all j values, compute max over j for each d __shared__ float s_max[96]; // D __shared__ float s_red[128]; // blockDim.x __shared__ float s_node_i[96]; // D, for residual // Load node_i for residual for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { s_node_i[d] = nodes[(b * N + i) * D + d]; s_max[d] = -FLT_MAX; } __syncthreads(); // Max reduction over j for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float max_val = -FLT_MAX; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float val = out[((b * N + i) * N + j) * D + d]; if (val > max_val) { max_val = val; } } s_max[d] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); // LayerNorm (same as before) ... // Add residual and write for (int d = tid; d < D; d += blockDim.x) { float v = s_max[d]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; float o = norm * ln_w[d] + ln_b[d]; o += s_node_i[d]; out[(b * N + i) * D + d] = o; } } ``` Wait, but the second kernel has the same low occupancy issue (only 96 blocks). However, its workload is much lighter: it's just a max over 24 values and a LayerNorm. Total compute of second kernel per block: - Max over j: D * N = 96 * 24 = 2304 comparisons - LayerNorm: mean/var over D = 96, then normalize This is very small. But since it reads from global memory (the output of first kernel), and there are 96 blocks, memory bandwidth might be the bottleneck for this kernel. First kernel write: 4*24*24*96*4 = 884,736 bytes ≈ 0.85MB Second kernel read: same amount With 3.35 TB/s bandwidth, 0.85MB takes ~0.25 microseconds. But with launch overhead and kernel underutilization, it might take longer. Actually, wait. Both kernels together involve: 1. First kernel: reads nodes, pair_feats, edge_mask, weights; writes fused 2. Second kernel: reads fused; writes final output Total memory moved: - nodes: B*N*D*4 = 4*24*96*4 = 36,864 bytes (read twice) - pair_feats: B*N*N*P*4 = 4*24*24*16*4 = 147,456 bytes (read once) - edge_mask: B*N*N*4 = 9,216 bytes (read once/twice) - weights: ~234KB (read in first kernel) - fused intermediate: ~885KB (write + read) - output: B*N*D*4 = 36,864 bytes Total main memory traffic: ~36KB + 147KB + 9KB + 234KB + 885KB + 37KB ≈ 1.35MB This is very small. The main cost is likely instruction execution and kernel launch overhead. For the single-kernel approach, we don't write fused to global memory, saving ~885KB of memory traffic. Wait, this is wrong. `-FLT_MAX` and `FLT_MAX` are symmetric in floating point: - FLT_MAX has binary representation with all exponent bits 1 except LSB (which is for infinity) and all mantissa bits 1. - -FLT_MAX is the same with sign bit flipped. So `-FLT_MAX + FLT_MAX = 0` exactly in floating point, because addition of a number and its exact negation gives 0. But our code computes `(-FLT_MAX) - (-FLT_MAX)` which is adding `-FLT_MAX` and `+FLT_MAX` in the arithmetic (since subtracting a negative is adding). So yes, it gives 0 exactly. Now, let me implement the pre-loaded shared memory version. ```cuda #define BLOCK_SIZE 128 __global__ void fused_scene_graph_kernel( const float* __restrict__ nodes, const float* __restrict__ pair_feats, const float* __restrict__ edge_mask, const float* __restrict__ w1_T, const float* __restrict__ b1, const float* __restrict__ w2_T, const float* __restrict__ b2, const float* __restrict__ ln_w, const float* __restrict__ ln_b, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int N, int D, int P, int H) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.x; const int i = blockIdx.y; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_node_i = s_mem; // [D] float* s_nodes_j = s_node_i + D; // [N * D] float* s_pairs = s_nodes_j + N * D; // [N * P] float* s_z1 = s_pairs + N * P; // [H] float* s_max = s_z1 + H; // [D] float* s_red = s_max + D; // [BLOCK_SIZE] // Load node_i for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_node_i[idx] = nodes[(b * N + i) * D + idx]; } // Load all nodes_j for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { int j = idx / D; int d = idx % D; s_nodes_j[idx] = nodes[(b * N + j) * D + d]; } // Load all pair_feats for (int idx = tid; idx < N * P; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { int j = idx / P; int p = idx % P; s_pairs[idx] = pair_feats[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + p]; } __syncthreads(); // Initialize max for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_max[idx] = -FLT_MAX; } __syncthreads(); const int L = 2 * D + P; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N + i) * N + j)]; // First linear + SiLU for (int h = tid; h < H; h += BLOCK_SIZE) { float acc = b1[h]; // node_i part #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { acc += w1_T[k * H + h] * s_node_i[k]; } // node_j part float* node_j_ptr = s_nodes_j + j * D; #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { acc += w1_T[(D + k) * H + h] * node_j_ptr[k]; } // pair part float* pair_ptr = s_pairs + j * P; #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < P; ++k) { acc += w1_T[(2 * D + k) * H + h] * pair_ptr[k]; } float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); s_z1[h] = acc * sig; } __syncthreads(); // Second linear, mask, and max for (int d = tid; d < D; d += BLOCK_SIZE) { float val = b2[d]; #pragma unroll 4 for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { val += w2_T[h * D + d] * s_z1[h]; } if (mask_val <= 0.5f) { val = -FLT_MAX; } if (val > s_max[d]) { s_max[d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); } // LayerNorm (mean and variance) float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { local_sum += s_max[idx]; } s_red[tid] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int stride = BLOCK_SIZE / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_red[tid] += s_red[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_red[0] / D; float local_var = 0.0f; for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { float diff = s_max[idx] - mean; local_var += diff * diff; } s_red[tid] = local_var; __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int stride = BLOCK_SIZE / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_red[tid] += s_red[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = s_red[0] / D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); // Final output for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { float v = s_max[idx]; float norm = (v - mean) * inv_std; float o = norm * ln_w[idx] + ln_b[idx]; o += s_node_i[idx]; out[(b * N + i) * D + idx] = o; } } ``` Wait, I should note that `#pragma unroll 4` on loops of varying sizes might not be ideal. For the node_i part with D=96, 96/4=24, so it's fine. For node_j part with D=96, same. For pair part with P=16, 16/4=4, fine. For w2 loop with H=192, 192/4=48, fine. But actually the compiler might not be able to unroll if the loop bound is dynamic (passed as argument). With `#pragma unroll 4`, it should partially unroll in chunks of 4 regardless. Hmm, but actually the loop bound `D`, `P`, `H` are kernel arguments, so they're not compile-time constants. The compiler might ignore `#pragma unroll` for dynamic bounds, or it might generate partial unrolling. Actually in CUDA, `#pragma unroll` works with dynamic bounds too - it just unrolls the body multiple times with a dynamic condition. One concern: in the first linear, I have three separate loops for node_i, node_j, and pair. This creates more instructions than a single loop over L. Let me see if a single loop is better: ```cpp for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { acc += w1_T[k * H + h] * s_node_i[k]; } for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { acc += w1_T[(D + k) * H + h] * s_nodes_j[j * D + k]; } for (int k = 0; k < P; ++k) { acc += w1_T[(2 * D + k) * H + h] * s_pairs[j * P + k]; } ``` vs ```cpp float* x[208]; // ... load addresses ... for (int k = 0; k < L; ++k) { acc += w1_T[k * H + h] * x[k]; } ``` The three-loop version avoids creating a contiguous buffer `s_x` but requires separate loops. Since we're computing per j, and the data is scattered across shared memory, the separate loops might actually be fine because they keep the accumulator in a register without needing to construct a contiguous array. Let me think about one more thing: when we access `s_nodes_j[j * D + k]`, `j` is fixed in the inner loop and `k` varies. Since all threads in a warp access the same `j`, they all access the same `s_nodes_j + j * D` base. Then `k` varies, so threads in a warp access different `k` offsets. But since each thread is computing a different `h`, not a different `k`, and all threads in a warp execute the same `k` sequentially, the access pattern for `s_nodes_j` is actually that ALL threads in a warp read the SAME `s_nodes_j[j * D + k]` for a given `k`. This is a broadcast and hits the same shared memory bank. Actually no - all threads in a warp access the SAME `k` at the SAME time (because they're all executing the same instruction in SIMD). So they all try to read the same shared memory address `s_nodes_j[j * D + k]`. This is handled by a broadcast from shared memory and is very fast! Similarly for `s_node_i[k]`, all threads read the same address - also a broadcast. For `s_pairs[j * P + k]`, same thing. For `w1_T[(D+k)*H + h]`, each thread reads a different `h`, so adjacent threads read adjacent memory - coalesced global memory access. This access pattern is actually very good! The broadcast pattern for the input data is optimal for shared memory. Great, this design is looking solid. Let me implement and test it. Actually, I realize there's another issue. I said "all threads in a warp" and I assumed block_size=128 means 4 warps, and threads within a warp are executing the same instructions. But in CUDA, threads within a warp execute in lockstep (SIMT). Since all threads in a warp are in the same `h` loop iteration (because they all iterate h += block_size starting from different offsets), they might NOT be on the same `h` value at the same time... wait. Actually, within a warp (32 threads), thread IDs are consecutive. With block_size=128, the threads in warp 0 have IDs 0-31. In the loop: ```cpp for (int h = tid; h < H; h += BLOCK_SIZE) { ... } ``` For warp 0: - tid=0: h=0, 128, ... (but H=192, so only h=0) - tid=1: h=1, 129, ... (only h=1) - ... - tid=31: h=31, 159, ... Wait, H=192, block_size=128. For tid=0: h=0, then 128 (which is < 192), so TWO iterations. For tid=64: h=64, then 192 (not < 192), so ONE iteration. Oh! Some threads do 2 iterations, some do 1. This causes divergence within the warp. For tid=0: h=0, h=128 (2 iterations) For tid=1: h=1, h=129 (2 iterations) ... For tid=63: h=63, h=191 (2 iterations) For tid=64: h=64 (1 iteration) ... For tid=127: h=127 (1 iteration) So warp 0 (threads 0-31) has 2 iterations for all threads. Warp 1 (threads 32-63) has 2 iterations. Warp 2 (threads 64-95) has 1 iteration. Actually, since ALL threads in warps 0 and 1 do exactly 2 iterations, there's NO divergence WITHIN those warps. The divergence is BETWEEN warps (which is fine since warps are independent!). Wait, I miscounted. Warp 0 = threads 0-31, all with tid < 64, so all do 2 iterations. Warp 1 = threads 32-63, all do 2 iterations. Warp 2 = threads 64-95, all do 1 iteration. Warp 3 = threads 96-127, all do 1 iteration. There's no divergence within any warp. Divergence between warps is natural and doesn't cause stalls. Wait, but with w1_T in shared memory, we need to load it once at the beginning. That's 160KB / (128 bytes per load per warp) = 160KB / (32*4 bytes per coalesced load) ≈ 1250 coalesced loads. Shared memory layout: ``` float* s_w1 = s_mem; // [L * H] float* s_node_i = s_w1 + L * H; // [D] float* s_nodes_j = s_node_i + D; // [N * D] float* s_pairs = s_nodes_j + N * D; // [N * P] float* s_z1 = s_pairs + N * P; // [H] float* s_max = s_z1 + H; // [D] float* s_red = s_max + D; // [BLOCK_SIZE] ``` Total = L*H + D + N*D + N*P + H + D + BLOCK_SIZE = 208*192 + 96 + 2304 + 384 + 192 + 96 + 192 = 39936 + 96 + 2304 + 384 + 192 + 96 + 192 = 43200 floats = 172800 bytes = 168.75KB Fits in 227KB! (Actually 228KB = 233472 bytes, and 172800 < 233472) Wait, but L*H = 208*192 = 39936 floats. Let me double check: L = 2*96 + 16 = 208 H = 192 L*H = 39936 floats = 159744 bytes Plus other: (96 + 2304 + 384 + 192 + 96 + 192) * 4 = 3364 * 4 = 13456 bytes Total = 159744 + 13456 = 173200 bytes = 169.14KB Yes, this fits! But wait, do I have enough shared memory to also load w2_T? w2_T = H*D = 192*96 = 18432 floats = 73728 bytes Total with w2_T = 173200 + 73728 = 246928 bytes = 241KB This exceeds 228KB! Actually, w1_T and w2_T are accessed the same number of times (once per j). The amount of data read per j is: - w1_T: 192 * 208 = 39936 floats - w2_T: 96 * 192 = 18432 floats So w1_T has more data but same number of accesses. The per-j bandwidth for w1_T is 39936 * 4 bytes = 159KB per j, or 24 * 159KB = 3.8MB total per block. w2_T is 1.8MB total per block. At 3.35TB/s, reading w1_T from global 24 times takes ~1.1 microseconds total. Reading w2_T takes ~0.5 microseconds. If we can cache w1_T in shared memory, we save ~1.1 microseconds. This seems worthwhile! But we also need to PAY for loading w1_T into shared memory once. That's 160KB from global to shared: 160KB / 3.35TB/s ≈ 0.05 microseconds. Very cheap compared to 24 re-reads! So pre-loading w1_T to shared memory is clearly a win. Let me implement this. Updated kernel: ```cuda #define BLOCK_SIZE 192 __global__ void fused_scene_graph_kernel(...) { const int tid = threadIdx.x; const int b = blockIdx.x; const int i = blockIdx.y; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_w1 = s_mem; // [L * H] float* s_node_i = s_w1 + L * H; // [D] float* s_nodes_j = s_node_i + D; // [N * D] float* s_pairs = s_nodes_j + N * D; // [N * P] float* s_z1 = s_pairs + N * P; // [H] float* s_max = s_z1 + H; // [D] float* s_red = s_max + D; // [BLOCK_SIZE] // Load w1_T to shared memory for (int idx = tid; idx < L * H; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_w1[idx] = w1_T[idx]; } // Load node_i for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_node_i[idx] = nodes[(b * N + i) * D + idx]; } // Load all nodes_j for (int idx = tid; idx < N * D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { int j = idx / D; int d = idx % D; s_nodes_j[idx] = nodes[(b * N + j) * D + d]; } // Load all pair_feats for (int idx = tid; idx < N * P; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { int j = idx / P; int p = idx % P; s_pairs[idx] = pair_feats[((b * N + i) * N + j) * P + p]; } __syncthreads(); // Initialize max for (int idx = tid; idx < D; idx += BLOCK_SIZE) { s_max[idx] = -FLT_MAX; } __syncthreads(); for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float mask_val = edge_mask[((b * N + i) * N + j)]; // First linear + SiLU for (int h = tid; h < H; h += BLOCK_SIZE) { float acc = b1[h]; #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { acc += s_w1[k * H + h] * s_node_i[k]; } float* node_j_ptr = s_nodes_j + j * D; #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < D; ++k) { acc += s_w1[(D + k) * H + h] * node_j_ptr[k]; } float* pair_ptr = s_pairs + j * P; #pragma unroll 4 for (int k = 0; k < P; ++k) { acc += s_w1[(2 * D + k) * H + h] * pair_ptr[k]; } float sig = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-acc)); s_z1[h] = acc * sig; } __syncthreads(); // Second linear, mask, and max for (int d = tid; d < D; d += BLOCK_SIZE) { float val = b2[d]; #pragma unroll 4 for (int h = 0; h < H; ++h) { val += w2_T[h * D + d] * s_z1[h]; } if (mask_val <= 0.5f) { val = -FLT_MAX; } if (val > s_max[d]) { s_max[d] = val; } } __syncthreads(); } // LayerNorm... } ``` The total shared memory now: L*H + D + N*D + N*P + H + D + BLOCK_SIZE = 39936 + 96 + 2304 + 384 + 192 + 96 + 192 = 43200 floats = 172800 bytes We need to set this in the kernel launch. Also, since w1_T is now in shared memory, the access `s_w1[k * H + h]` is a shared memory access. With h varying by thread, adjacent threads access adjacent shared memory locations (bank 0, 1, 2, 3, ...). This has no bank conflicts! For the node_i broadcast: `s_node_i[k]` is the same address for all threads in a warp (since k is the loop variable, same for all threads at each step). This is a broadcast with no bank conflicts. For node_j: `s_nodes_j[j * D + k]`, j is fixed for the iteration and k is the loop variable. All threads access the same address at each step. Broadcast, no bank conflicts. For pairs: same, broadcast, no bank conflicts. For w2_T: `w2_T[h * D + d]` with d varying by thread. Coalesced global memory, no conflicts. This access pattern is almost ideal! One small thing: in the h-loop with block_size=192 and H=192, each thread has exactly 1 iteration (h = tid). No loop overhead! This is great. For the d-loop with block_size=192 and D=96, threads 0..95 have h = tid, which is < 96, so 1 iteration. Threads 96..191 have h >= 96, so 0 iterations. One caveat: I should make sure to compile and test. Let me do that. Wait, I also need to double-check my pre-load indexing for w1_T. w1_T has shape (L, H) = (208, 192). In shared memory, I put it as s_w1[idx] = w1_T[idx], which is row-major. So s_w1[k * H + h] accesses the same element as w1_T[k, h]. This matches my existing access pattern. Good! Let me also consider whether to pre-load b1, b2, ln_w, ln_b to shared memory.
